The Definition of Sound and Its Confusion with Causes
Author introduces audibles and defines sound, refuting misconceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essence of sound is intuitive and distinct from its physical causes.

Core Idea
Sound's essence is self-evident, not to be confused with its causes.

This passage from the discussion of audibles clarifies that sound, despite being intuitively known, has been mistakenly identified with its causes—the wave of air, or the strike or split. The author defends the intuitive nature of sound and warns against conflating it with its physical causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview. Notice the structure: introduction, two sections, then the first aim on sound.
The text ends abruptly with 'ربما'; the author's argument continues, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the proposed definitions
The passage mentions two incorrect definitions: sound as the wave of air, and sound as the strike or split. Understand why these are considered causes, not the essence.
The author calls the wave the 'proximate cause' and the strike/split 'remote causes'.
3
Understand the author's correction
The author asserts that sound's quiddity is intuitive and needs no definition. He supports this by noting that the wave is perceived by touch, not hearing, showing that sound is something else.
This highlights the importance of distinguishing between a thing and its causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : المسموعات من المحسوسات المسموعات وهي الأصوات والحروف التي هي كيفيات عارضة للأصوات ومباحثه قسمان القسم الأول : في الصوت قدمه على الحرف لكونه معروضاً له متقدماً عليه بالطبع وفيه مقاصد المقصد الأول : الصوت هو التموج إن الصوت وإن كان بديهي التصور كسائر المحسوسات إلا أنه قد اشتبهت عند بعضهم ماهيته بسببه فقيل : الصوت هو التموج أي تموج الهواء وهو سببه القريب وقيل : الصوت هو القرع أو القلع مع أن هذين سببان له بعيدان والحق كما أشرنا إليه أن ماهيته بديهية مستغنية عن التعريف ومغايرة لما توهموه فإن التموج محسوس باللمس ألا أن یری الصوت الشديد ربما
The third type: audible things. Among perceptibles are audible things, namely sounds and letters (ḥurūf) which are qualities accidental to sounds. Its investigations are two sections. The first section: on sound. He prefaced it over the letter because it is a substrate for it, prior to it by nature. Within it are aims. The first aim: sound is the wave (al-tamawwuj). Indeed, sound, although its conception is intuitive like other perceptibles, its quiddity has been confused by some due to its cause. So it was said: sound is the wave, i.e., the waving of air, which is its proximate cause. And it was said: sound is the strike (al-qarʿ) or the split (al-qalʿ), although these are two remote causes. The truth, as we have indicated, is that its quiddity is intuitive, needing no definition, and different from what they imagined, for the wave is perceived by touch, but you see that intense sound sometimes...

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion of the third type of perceptibles: audibles. The author first states that audibles include sounds and letters, where letters are qualities that occur to sounds. The investigation is divided into two sections, the first being about sound, which is given precedence because it serves as the substrate for the letter. The first aim within this section is to define sound. Despite sound being intuitively known, some have confused its essence with its cause. Two incorrect views are mentioned: that sound is the wave (tamawwuj) of air (which is actually its proximate cause), and that sound is the strike or split (which are remote causes). The author affirms that the truth is that the essence of sound is self-evident, needing no definition, and is distinct from these causes. He supports this by noting that the wave is perceptible by touch, not by hearing, showing that sound is something else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound is the wave of air.
Correction: The wave of air is the proximate cause of sound, not sound itself. Sound's essence is intuitively known and is perceived by hearing, while the wave is perceived by touch.

Two Empirical Proofs That Hearing Requires Air to Reach the Ear
Sanusi provides two empirical proofs for the necessity of air reaching the ear for hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two empirical demonstrations that hearing sound depends on the physical arrival of the air carrying it at the earhole.

Core Idea
Sound does not travel magically; it needs air to reach the ear.

Sanusi offers two concrete experiments—one with a tube, one with wind—to show that hearing is not an abstract relation but requires the air medium to physically arrive at the ear. These proofs ground the theoretical claim in everyday experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the careful wording of the two proofs.
2
Understand the tube experiment
Visualize the tube: speaker at one end, listener at the other. Why does only that person hear? Because the tube confines the air.
3
Understand the wind experiment
Think of the mu'adhdhin's voice: downwind hears, upwind does not. Why? The wind moves the air carrying the sound.
4
Connect to the general principle
Both proofs show that hearing requires the air to arrive at the ear. This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إن الاحساس بالصوت يتوقف على وصول الهواء الحامل له إلى حاسة مة السامع لوجوه الأول : أنَّ من وضع فمه في طرف أنبوبة طويلة ووضع طرفها الآخر في صماخ إنسان وتكلم فيه بصوت عال سمعه ذلك الإنسان دون غيره من الحاضرين وإن كانوا أقرب إلى المتكلم من ذلك الإنسان وما هو إلا لحصرها أي ليس سماعه للصوت دون غيره إلا لحصر الأنبوبة الهواء الحامل للصوت ومنعها إياه من الانتشار والوصول إلى صماخ الغير فلا يصل إلا إلى صماخ ذلك الإنسان فلا يسمعه إلا هو . الثاني : أنه أعني الصوت يميل مع الريح كما هو المجرب في صوت المؤذن على المنارة فمن كان منه في جهة تهب الريح إليها يسمع صوته وإن كان بعيداً ومن كان في غير تلك الجهة لا يسمعه وإن تساويا في مسافة البعد وما ذلك إلا لأن الريح تميل الهواء الحامل له وتحركه إلى الجانب الذي هبت إليه فدل على أنَّ
And we only said that the sensation of sound depends on the arrival of the air carrying it to the sense of hearing, due to proofs: First, that if a person places his mouth at one end of a long tube and places its other end in the earhole of another person and speaks in it with a loud voice, that person hears it without others present, even if they are closer to the speaker than that person; and it is only due to the tube confining the air carrying the sound and preventing it from spreading and reaching the earhole of others, so it only reaches the earhole of that person, so only he hears it. Second, that sound inclines with the wind, as is experienced with the voice of the mu'adhdhin on a minaret: so whoever is in a direction towards which the wind blows hears his voice, even if far away, and whoever is not in that direction does not hear it, even if they are equal in distance; and that is only because the wind inclines the air carrying the sound and moves it to the side towards which it blew. Thus it indicates that...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents two empirical proofs to support the claim that hearing sound requires the actual arrival of the air carrying it at the earhole. The first proof uses a tube: when someone speaks into one end of a long tube held to another person's ear, only that person hears, because the tube confines the air and prevents it from dispersing to others. If sound could be heard without the air reaching the ear, others nearby would also hear it. The second proof invokes the common experience of a mu'adhdhin's voice on a minaret: the sound is heard downwind but not upwind, even at equal distances, because the wind moves the air carrying the sound. Both proofs demonstrate that the sensation of sound is tied to the physical arrival of the air medium at the ear, not to some abstract relation. The conclusion (indicated by 'فدل على أنَّ') is that hearing depends on the arrival of the air.

Key Terms
أنبوبة
صماخ
ريح
مؤذن
منارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think sound travels independently of the air medium.
Correction: These proofs show that hearing requires the actual arrival of the air carrying the sound at the earhole.

Checkpoint
What does the tube experiment in this passage demonstrate?
A) Sound travels faster in tubes.
B) Sound can be heard without air reaching the earhole.
C) Sound is heard only when the air carrying it reaches the earhole.
D) Tubes amplify sound.

Key Takeaway
Hearing sound depends on the actual arrival of the air carrying the sound to the ear, as shown by the tube and wind experiments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'air' (means of connection) have you neglected in your relationship with Allah, causing His guidance not to reach your heart? How will you actively bring that medium back today?
?
When you recite or listen to the Qur'an, do you ensure that the 'air' of presence and understanding reaches your ear and heart, or are you just hearing words without the medium of reflection? What will you change in your next recitation?
?
If you were to test your own spiritual hearing like the tube experiment, what barriers (like distractions or sins) are blocking the air of revelation from reaching your inner ear? Name one barrier you will remove this week.

Objection and Response: The Wind Proof Refined
Objection and refutation concerning the wind proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a specific objection to the wind proof is refuted by showing that the disturbance of hearing actually confirms the dependence of sensation on air arrival.

Core Idea
Disturbance in hearing proves the very dependence it was meant to disprove.

The author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif raises an empirical counterexample: when wind blows, we hear the mu'adhdhin's voice moving away, yet we are in a windless spot. He claims this shows sound reaches the ear without the air carrier. The author responds by noting that our hearing is disturbed, which indicates that the air must have reached our ear but with interference. This turns the objection into further evidence for the original proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay close attention to the objector's empirical claim: that we hear a sound even though the air carrying it does not reach our ear because we are in a windless location.
The objector uses a common experience: the mu'adhdhin's voice seems to shift direction with the wind.
2
Identify the response
Locate the phrase 'وفيه نظر' (There is a consideration). Read the response and notice the key point: the disturbance (تشوش) of hearing indicates that the air did reach the ear, but with interference.
The response uses the very fact of disturbance as evidence for dependence.
3
Reflect on the logic
Think about why the disturbance proves dependence. If hearing did not depend on air arrival, the sound would remain clear regardless of wind. The fact that it becomes disturbed shows that the air carrier is indeed necessary.
This turns the objection into a supportive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعترض عليه صاحب الصحائب بأنا ندرك أن صوت المؤذن عند هبوب الرياح يميل عن جهتنا إلى خلافها، وذلك ضروري يعرفه كل أحد بالتجربة ومن المعلوم ضرورة أنّ الهواء الحامل لذلك الصوت ما وصل إلى صماخنا إذ نحن وقتئذ في موضع لا ريح فيه حتى يقال : إنه صرفه عن جهتنا بل كان خارجاً عن ذلك الموضع صرفه الريح عن جهتنا فقد سمعنا صوتا مع عدم وصول الهواء الحاصل لذلك الصوت إلى صماخنا، وفيه نظر لأن تشوش سماع الصوت حينئذ يدل على وصول الهواء الحامل له إلى صماخنا إذ لو لم يكن الإحساس متوقفاً على ذلك الوصول لما تشوش ضرورة والتالي باطل بالتجربة فكذا المقدم
The author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif objected: We perceive that the voice of the mu'adhdhin when the winds blow inclines from our direction to opposite it, and this is known necessarily by everyone through experience; and it is necessarily known that the air carrying that sound did not reach our earhole, because we are at that time in a place where there is no wind, so it cannot be said that it directed it away from our direction; rather it was outside that place, the wind directed it away from our direction. So we heard a sound without the air carrying that sound reaching our earhole. There is a consideration in this: because the disturbance of hearing the sound at that time indicates the arrival of the air carrying it to our earhole; for if sensation were not dependent on that arrival, it would not have necessarily been disturbed, and the consequent is false by experience, so the antecedent is also false.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif against the second proof (wind proof). The objector argues that when wind blows, we hear the mu'adhdhin's voice moving away from us; since we are in a wind-free location, the air carrying the sound could not have reached our earhole. Yet we still hear, so hearing does not require air arrival. The response (وفيه نظر) refutes this by pointing out that our hearing of the sound is disturbed (tashawwush) – we hear it poorly or indistinctly. This disturbance actually proves that hearing depends on the air's arrival, because if it were not dependent, the sound would remain clear irrespective of wind. The disturbance is empirical evidence for the conclusion, so the objection fails and actually supports the original claim.

Key Terms

ريح
تشوش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that hearing does not depend on air reaching the ear.
Correction: The disturbance of hearing actually proves the opposite: if hearing were independent, it would not be disturbed; disturbance indicates interference with the air carrier, confirming its role.

Checkpoint

B) He argues that the disturbance in hearing proves that sensation depends on air arrival.
C) He claims that the wind does not affect sound propagation.
D) He denies the experience of the mu'adhdhin.

Key Takeaway
The disturbance of hearing in the wind experiment confirms that sensation depends on the arrival of the air carrier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the wind disturbs your hearing of the adhan, do you ever pause to consider that it is Allah's design that makes the sound reach you? How could this awareness change your experience of hearing the call to prayer?

?
The disturbance in hearing was proof of dependence. What 'disturbances' in your spiritual life—difficulties, doubts, trials—might actually be evidence of Allah's intricate design? Ask yourself: Are you willing to let these disturbances drive you closer to Him rather than away?

Defending the Tube Proof: Objection and Response
Al-Sanusi presents an objection to the tube proof and gives a two-part response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the tube proof for the necessity of air in hearing is defended against an objection that invokes an alternative explanation of sense detachment.

Core Idea
The tube proof holds because we deny the objection's premises and note possible missing conditions.

The tube proof argues that a tube prevents sound from reaching the ear by confining the air. But an objector suggests the tube might block the sense of hearing itself, like vision. In this passage, al-Sanusi presents and refutes that objection, defending the proof's conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for its overall structure, then read the translation. Notice the objection (قد يقال) and the response (على أنا لا نسلم...).
The response has two parts: denial of the premise and invocation of another condition.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says the tube might block hearing directly, like it blocks vision, so the tube does not prove air is necessary. Identify the analogy with sight.
This challenges the necessity of air arrival.
3
Analyze the first response
The response first denies that air does not reach the ears: 'we do not concede that air does not reach the earholes of those present.' This rebuts the objection's assumption.
4
Analyze the second response
Even if air reached, the lack of hearing could be due to another missing condition (sharṭ). So the objection does not refute the proof.
This shields the proof from the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وما هو إلا لحصرها الخ قد يقال : لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك بمنع الأنبوبة أن يتعلق حاسة السمع بالصوت الذي في داخلها كما يمنع حاسة البصر مع رؤية ما في داخلها إذا كان فيه شيء مرئي فلا يفيد توقف الإحساس بالصوت على وصول الهواء الحامل إلى الصماخ على أنا لا نسلم عدم وصول الهواء إلى صماخ الحاضرين ولو قيل : لو وصل لسمع يمنع لجواز توقفه على شرط آخر
His statement: 'and it is only due to confining it etc.' It may be said: It is not impossible that this is due to the tube preventing the sense of hearing from attaching to the sound inside it, just as it prevents the sense of sight from seeing what is inside it when there is something visible in it; so it does not prove the dependence of sensation of sound on the arrival of the carrying air to the earhole. However, we do not concede that the air does not reach the earholes of those present; and if it were said: if it reached, it would have been heard, it is prevented because it may depend on another condition.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the tube proof. The objector suggests that the tube might work not by physically confining air, but by preventing the sense of hearing from directly attaching to the distant sound, analogous to how a tube blocks vision of an object inside it. Therefore, the tube experiment does not prove that hearing requires air arrival. The response has two parts: first, we do not concede that the air does not reach the ears of the listeners (the tube does not block air completely; some air may still exit). Second, even if one argues that if air reached they would hear, the lack of hearing could be due to another condition (sharṭ) that is absent. Thus, the objection does not refute the proof.

Key Terms
أنبوبة
حاسة السمع
حاسة البصر
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tube proof is unassailable and cannot be questioned.
Correction: The objection raises a plausible alternative explanation, but the response skillfully defends the proof by denying the factual premise and invoking other possible conditions.

Checkpoint
What is the first part of the response to the tube objection?
A) We concede that air does not reach the ears.
B) We do not concede that air does not reach the ears.
C) The tube blocks hearing directly.
D) The sound is inaudible.

Key Takeaway
The tube proof survives the objection because we can deny its factual premise and allow for other necessary conditions besides air.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When an objection to your belief is presented, are you quick to accept its conclusion or do you, like Al-Sanusi, examine whether its premises are really true? Will you commit to questioning the factual claims of objections you encounter this week?
?
This lesson shows that an argument can survive an objection by denying a false premise. Is there an area in your life where you have conceded a premise that is actually false, out of politeness or lack of knowledge? Will you re-examine that premise today and see if it stands?
?
The response also allows that another condition may be missing. When your heart feels distant from faith, do you assume the cause is simply a lack of knowledge, or could there be another missing condition (sincere reflection, humility, repentance) that you have not fulfilled? Will you ask yourself what that condition might be and act on it?

Two Proofs That Sound Exists Outside the Ear
Two proofs from direction and distance that sound exists outside the ear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs from perception of direction and distance that sound exists outside the ear, and how these proofs relate to the condition of sound's arrival.

Core Idea
We perceive direction and distance of sound, proving it exists at a location before reaching the ear.

This passage presents two arguments that sound is not merely a sensation in the ear but exists externally. By examining how we discern where a sound comes from and whether it is near or far, the author demonstrates that sound must have an objective existence beyond the ear canal. These proofs challenge the notion that sound is only perceived upon arrival at the ear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Note the two proofs presented.
The passage has some technical terms like الصماخ (ear canal).
2
Understand the first proof from direction
The author argues that we perceive the direction of sounds, so sound must exist before reaching the ear and be perceived at its source location to distinguish its direction.
The author also clarifies that this does not require sound to be far; even close sounds are considered 'arriving'.
3
Understand the second proof from distance
We distinguish between near and far sounds. This would be impossible if sound only existed at the ear; it must be present at different distances outside the ear.
This proof seems to contradict the common condition that hearing requires the arrival of the air wave.
4
Notice the author's qualification on 'arrival'
The author notes that the second proof apparently contradicts the condition of sensation by arrival, setting up a tension to be resolved later.
This shows the author is engaging with a well-known objection.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how both proofs together establish sound's external existence. Write down your own explanation of the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الثالث : الصوت موجود خارج الصماخ
ويصل ذلك الشيء إليها ) لا في مسافة لم يتميز عندنا بلمس اليد ( جهته ) أي جهة ذلك الشيء الملموس ولم ندر أنه من أي جهة أتانا لكنا ندرك في بعض الأوقات جهات الأصوات فوجب أن يكون الصوت موجوداً قبل الوصول إلى السامعة وأن يكون مدركاً هناك أيضاً لتميز جهته وليس يلزم أن يكون حينئذ بعيداً عنا [ليناً؟] في ما أن تقدم من أن الإحساس بالصوت مشروط بوصول الهواء الحامل له إلينا بل يجوز يكون [قريباًمنا] جداً فيكون واصلاً إلينا ؛ إذ لم نرد بالوصول حقيقته بل ما يتناولها، وما في حكمها من القرب ( ولذلك ) أي ولأن الصوت موجود في خارج الصماخ (تميز بين الصوت ( القريب و الصوت ( البعيد ) إذ لولا أن الأصوات موجودة في خارج الأصمخة ومدركة حيث هي من الأمكنة لما أمكننا أن نميز بينها بحسب القرب والبعد . وهذا الدليل الثاني لابتنائه على إدراك الصوت في مكانه القريب أو البعيد من السامع ينافي بظاهرة اشتراط الإحساس بالوصول
The third type - first section: third goal: sound exists outside the ear canal. And that thing reaches it, not in a distance that is distinguished for us by touch of the hand. Its direction, i.e. the direction of that touched thing, and we did not know from which direction it came to us, but we perceive at some times the directions of sounds. So it is necessary that sound exists before reaching the hearing faculty and that it is perceived there as well to distinguish its direction. And it is not necessary that it then be far from us, soft? in what has preceded, that sensation of sound is conditioned by the arrival of the air carrying it to us. Rather, it may be very close to us, so it reaches us, since we did not intend by 'arrival' its reality, but what includes it and what is in its ruling of closeness. (And therefore) i.e., because sound exists outside the ear canal, we distinguish between near sound and far sound. For if sounds were not existing outside the ear canals and perceived where they are from the places, we could not distinguish between them according to nearness and farness. And this is the second proof, because it is based on the perception of sound in its place near or far from the hearer, which apparently contradicts the condition of sensation by arrival.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage defines the position that sound exists outside the ear and provides two proofs. The first proof is based on our ability to perceive the direction of sounds: we often know from which direction a sound comes, so sound must exist before it reaches the ear and must be perceived at its location for its direction to be distinguished. The author clarifies that this does not require the sound to be far away; it can be very close and still be considered reaching us. The second proof is based on our ability to distinguish between near and far sounds. If sounds were not present outside the ear and perceived at their locations, we could not make this distinction. The author then notes that this second proof apparently contradicts the well-known condition that hearing requires the arrival of the air carrying the sound, because it implies that the far sound is perceived at its distant location. The author thus sets up a problem to be addressed.

Key Takeaway

Will you commit today to using your hearing for good, listening to truth and avoiding falsehood, as a way of showing gratitude?

Objection from al-Mu'tabar and the Response
Objection to direct sound perception and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tabar's objection that perceiving sound direction requires tracing the effect of the undulating air backward, and the author's alternative explanation involving the wave in the ear tube.

Core Idea
Sound direction is perceived by tracing the wave effect, not by sound existing at the source.

The author of al-Mu'tabar raises a subtle objection against the idea that direction is perceived directly from a sound existing outside. He argues that we only immediately perceive the sound in the ear after the air strikes, and then we trace it backward to infer direction. The author offers a different mechanism involving the wave in the ear tube. This lesson unpacks this debate and the commentator's clarifications, showing the scientific nature of the inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from al-Mu'tabar
Read the Arabic passage carefully and follow the objector's reasoning: perception occurs first by air striking the ear, then we trace the effect back.
Notice the distinction between immediate perception and derived awareness of direction.
2
Understand the author's response
The author offers an alternative: the wave in the external ear tube creates a state that causes direction perception without a subsisting sound outside. He admits this is not certain.
3
Analyze the commentator's clarifications
The commentator explains two points: first, direction perception does not require the sound to be far; second, the apparent contradiction about 'arrival' is only apparent.
Reflect on how these clarifications strengthen the author's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن قال صاحب المعتبر: إنا قد علمنا أن هذا الإدراك إنما يحصل أولاً بقرع الهواء المتموج لتجويف الصماخ ولذلك يصل من الأبعد في زمان أطول لكن بمجرد إدراكنا الصوت القائم بالهواء القارع للصماخ لا يحصل لنا الشعور بالجهة والقرب والبعد بل ذلك إنما يحصل بتتبع الأثر الوارد من حيث ورد وتتبع ما بقي منه في الهواء الذي هو في المسافة التي فيها ورد قال : والحاصل أن عند غفلتنا يرد علينا هواء قارع فندرك الصوت الذي فيه عند الصماخ وهذا القدر لا يفيد إدراك الجهة، ثم إنا بعد ذلك نتتبعه بتأملنا فيتأدى إدراكنا من الذي وصل إلينا إلى ما قبله فما قبله من جهته ومبدأ وروده فإن كان بقي حيث تلقاه اتفاقاً فكيف لا يضر المعلل ؟ والحق أن احتمال إدراك الجهة بكون التموج في الأنبوبة الخارجة مبدأ لحالة يصير سبباً لذلك الإدراك من غير أن يكون هناك صوت قائم، وإن كان لا يخلو عن بعد . وقد اعترف بأن المسألة علمية يطلب فيها اليقين.
قوله : ( وليس يلزم أن يكون حينئذ الخ أي ليس يلزم في إدراك الجهة أن يكون الصوت حين إدراكه المفضي إلى إدراك جهته بعيداً لأن إدراكه حال قربه يفيد إدراك جهته وإن كان مبدؤه بعيداً في نفس الأمر فليتأمل. قوله: (ينافي بظاهرة اشتراط الإحساس بالوصول سواء حمل على الوصول حقيقة أو ما يتناوله وما في حكمه من القريب جداً وذلك لدلالته على أن الصوت البعيد يدرك حيث هو، وإنما قال بظاهره لإمكان أن يقال الوصول شرط إحساس البعيد بطريق التبع كما أشار إليه بنقل كلام صاحب المعتبر
But the author of al-Mu'tabar said: We have known that this perception only occurs first by the striking of the undulating air against the cavity of the ear canal, and therefore it reaches from the farther in a longer time. But by merely our perceiving the sound subsisting in the air striking the ear canal, we do not obtain awareness of direction and nearness and farness. Rather, that only occurs by tracing the effect that comes from where it came, and tracing what remains of it in the air that is in the distance from which it came. He said: The conclusion is that when we are heedless, striking air comes to us and we perceive the sound in it at the ear canal, and this much does not yield perception of direction. Then after that we trace it with our reflection, so our perception leads from what reached us to what preceded it, so what preceded it is its direction and the origin of its arrival. If something remained where it met it coincidentally, then how would it not harm the reasoner? But the truth is that the possibility of perceiving direction is by the wave in the external tube being the beginning of a state that becomes the cause of that perception without there being a subsisting sound, although it is not free from remoteness. And he admitted that the issue is scientific and requires certainty. His statement: 'And it is not necessary that it then be far' etc. means it is not necessary for perception of direction that the sound when perceived leading to perception of its direction be far, because its perception in its state of nearness yields perception of its direction even if its origin is far in reality, so reflect. His statement: 'It apparently contradicts the condition of sensation by arrival' whether interpreted as arrival in reality or as what includes it and what is in its ruling of very near, because it indicates that the far sound is perceived where it is. He only said 'apparently' because it is possible to say that arrival is a condition for sensation of the far in a derivative way, as he indicated by quoting the statement of the author of al-Mu'tabar.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the author of al-Mu'tabar (صاحب المعتبر) and the author's response, followed by the commentator's explanation. The objector argues that our perception of sound direction is not because sound exists outside the ear, but because we first perceive the sound in the ear after the air strikes it, and then we trace the effect backward through the air to its origin. We do not immediately perceive direction or distance; we only perceive the sound itself. Later, by reflection, we trace the effect and thus know the direction. The author responds by offering an alternative: it is possible that direction is perceived because the wave in the external ear tube creates a state that causes that perception, even if no sound exists there. He admits the matter is subtle and requires certainty. Then the commentator clarifies two points: first, that sound can be close when perceived even if its origin is far, so direction perception does not require the sound to be far. Second, the author's statement that the second proof apparently contradicts the condition of arrival is only apparent, because arrival can be interpreted in a broad sense including very close sounds, and the far sound might be perceived indirectly.

Key Terms
صاحب المعتبر
قرع الهواء
الأثر
التموج
الأنبوبة الخارجة
المعلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author fully rejects the objector's explanation and affirms a certain direct mechanism.
Correction: The author offers a different hypothesis but admits it is not free from difficulty and acknowledges the issue requires certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the objector (صاحب المعتبر), what is the basis of perceiving the direction of a sound?
A) The sound existing outside the ear at its source
B) The tracing of the effect of the striking air back to its origin
C) The intensity of the sound wave
D) The shape of the ear canal

Key Takeaway
The perception of sound direction relies on tracing the wave effect backward, not on the sound itself being at the source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a sound and automatically know its direction, do you ever stop to consider that this knowledge is not immediate but built on a chain of effects? How does this realization challenge your trust in the immediacy of your senses? Will you now take a moment to reflect on what your senses actually give you versus what your mind infers?
?
The author of al-Mu'tabar admitted that the issue requires certainty and is not free from difficulty. In your own life, are there beliefs about Allah or invisible realities that you hold with certainty without having traced the evidence? Will you commit to examining those beliefs more carefully, tracing them back to their sources like the sound is traced back to its origin?

Al-Razi's Objection and the Inference of Sound Direction
Objection and refutation regarding sound direction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's objection that hearing perceives only sound, not direction, and the multiple refutations provided in the text, including the analogy of smell and the role of effect strength.

Core Idea
Sound direction is inferred from the trace, not directly sensed.

The discussion turns to a critical objection by Imam al-Razi: how can we claim to perceive direction through hearing if hearing only registers sound itself? The author responds that just as we know a smell is in a body without directly sensing the body, we know a sound is in a direction without directly sensing direction. Further counter-objections and responses refine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial explanation
The passage begins by explaining how hearing perceives direction through tracing the effect of sound waves. Read the first part carefully, noting the examples of thunder vs. millstone and the speech of two men.
This sets the background for al-Razi's objection.
2
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues that hearing only perceives the sound itself, not its direction. Identify the core of his objection: direction is not a direct object of hearing.
He admits the tracing process but denies that it yields direction perception.
3
Analyze the response and analogy
The author refutes al-Razi by comparing sound to smell: we know a smell is in a body even though we don't perceive the body itself by smell. Similarly, we know sound is in a direction even if direction is not directly sensed.
This is a key analogical argument.
4
Consider the counter-objections
Two counter-objections are raised: first, that direction is known from the air current, not from sound location; second, that near/far distinction comes from effect strength, not from sound location. Note how these challenge the author's earlier two proofs.
5
Examine the grammatical note
The passage ends with a grammatical clarification about the phrase 'except by what remains' and a discussion of whether the cause of perceiving direction is limited to the two mentioned. This shows scholarly attention to textual precision.
This part may be more technical.

From it something traced we perceived to where it ends and perishes, and then we perceive the arriving and its origin and what remains of it existing, and its direction and the farness of its origin and its nearness, and what remains of the strength of its waves and their weakness. And if there remains in the distance no effect that alerts us to the beginning, we do not know the extent of distance except by what remains. Therefore we do not distinguish in distance between thunder reaching us from the upper atmosphere and the sound of a millstone that is closer to us, but we distinguish in it between the speech of two men we do not see, one being a cubit away and the other two cubits away. For when we hear their speech, we know the nearness of one and the farness of the other. Imam al-Razi said: This is the utmost of what has been said on this topic, but there remains a discussion: Suppose the hearer traces from what reached him to what preceded it, but the object of hearing is the sound itself, not the direction, for it is not perceived by hearing at all. And if direction is not perceived by it, then the sound being in that direction is not perceived by it. So it remains that what is perceived is the sound that is in that direction, not insofar as it is in that direction, but only insofar as it is sound. And this amount perceived by hearing does not differ with different directions, so it does not cause perception of direction at all. But its weakness is apparent, for if sound is perceived in a direction, one knows that it is in that direction, even if the direction itself and the fact that the sound is in it are not perceived by hearing. Do you not see that if a smell is perceived from a body, one knows that it is in it, even though the body itself and the fact that the smell is in it are not perceived by smell? (It cannot be said: We only perceive it by the direction from it, i.e., we only perceive the direction of sound because the air striking the ear canal came from that direction, not because sound exists in it, as you mentioned in the first proof. And we distinguish between near and far because the effect of the near is stronger than the effect of the far. For if the striking is near, the effect produced by it is stronger than the effect produced from the far. Therefore the near is distinguished from the far, not because sound exists outside the ear canal and is heard where it is from a near or far place, as you mentioned in the second proof). (Because we answer the first...) His statement: 'except by what remains' does not need to be made an interrupted exception because what is first negated is the remaining of the effect alerting to the beginning, not the absolute remaining of the effect, so reflect. His statement: 'For we answer the first' it was said: this is speech on the authority? And it was answered: that the restriction in his saying 'we only perceive it by the direction' indicates its equivalence to the prevention? And you are aware that that restriction is additive, as indicated by the commentator's statement 'not because sound exists in it', so it does not indicate its equivalence to the prevention. This is supported by what al-Katibi mentioned in Sharh al-Mulakhkhas, and the commentator brought it in his glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn without addressing what is upon it, where he said: And one may prevent the limitation of the cause of perceiving direction to the two mentioned, so that from the invalidity of one the other is necessarily determined.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion with several objections and answers. First, it elaborates on how the tracing of the effect allows us to distinguish between near and far sounds, using the example of thunder versus a millstone, and the speech of two men at different distances. Then Imam al-Razi raises an objection: even if we trace the sound, what is perceived by hearing is only the sound itself, not its direction or location. Therefore, the sound being in a certain direction does not cause perception of direction. The answer is that this is weak: when we perceive sound in a direction, we know it is in that direction, even if direction is not directly sensed—similar to how we perceive a smell from a body and know the smell is in it. Then another objection is raised: we perceive direction due to the air coming from that direction, not because sound is there, and we distinguish near and far by the strength of the effect, not by sound location. The answer that follows is cut off, but the commentator explains a point about the exception in the text and discusses the restriction of the cause of perceiving direction to the two mentioned, citing al-Katibi and the commentator of Hikmat al-Ayn.

Correction: Hearing perceives sound, but direction is inferred from the trace of the effect or through analogy with smell.

Key Takeaway
Direction of sound is known by inference from the trace of the effect or by analogy, not by direct sensory perception of hearing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the call to prayer, do you merely perceive the sound, or do you infer its direction with gratitude? How often do you reflect that your knowledge of the direction of the mosque is not directly sensed but inferred through the trace of the muezzin's voice? This mirrors how you know truths about Allah—not by direct sight, but through His signs. Are you as confident in those inferences as you are in the direction of a sound?
?
Al-Razi's objection stemmed from a philosophical doubt about indirect knowledge. In your own life, have you ever let skepticism about mediated knowledge undermine your certainty in matters of faith? What traces of Allah's existence and guidance have you perceived, and why do you trust them with the same natural acceptance you grant to the origin of a sound?

The Cause of Echo: New Undulation of Air
Sanusi defines the cause of echo as a new air undulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that echo is caused by a new undulation of air, not the return of the original air wave.

Core Idea
Echo is a fresh sound wave, not the original one bouncing back.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the physical cause of echo. He corrects a common misconception: echo is not the original sound wave returning, but a new wave generated by the impact of the first wave against a resistant body. This lesson unpacks that process and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on echo's cause
Read the opening section where Sanusi states the apparent (al-ẓāhir) view and describes the undulation process. Pay attention to the analogy of successive impacts.
2
Identify the misconception
Notice that the author mentions a mistaken belief: that the original air returns with its undulation. See how he refutes it using the observation that echo resembles the original sound in form and description.
This refutation relies on the listener noticing the similarity between the echo and the original sound.
3
Understand the physical mechanism
Trace the chain: initial air undulation → strikes a resistant body → new undulation arises → that new wave is the echo. The original undulation does not persist.
The new undulation is said to be 'similar to the first' but not identical in identity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does it matter that echo is a new wave? (It means echo is an independent sound event, not a reflection of the original sound.)
This distinction has implications for understanding sound propagation and auditory perception.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third type - the first section - the fourth objective: The undulating air that carries sound. (First: It is apparent that echo, that is, the cause of echo) is the undulation of new air, not the return of the first air. And that is because when the air undulates in the manner you have known, as previously mentioned, until the undulating part of it strikes a body that resists it and sends it back, that undulation which had occurred to it does not remain in the struck air. Rather, due to its striking and returning, an undulation similar to the first undulation occurs in it. So this new undulation resulting from the striking and returning is the cause of the echo that resembles the first sound. And just as the first undulation was by impact after impact and stillness after stillness, so is the case with the second undulation whose beginning was at the end of the first. And one might think that the struck air returns with its first undulation itself and thus carries that first sound to the hearer. Do you not see that the echo is upon its description and form? This, although possible, yet the first is apparent. (The second branch: Some have thought that every sound has an echo.) Imam al-Razi said: The most similar is that, because when air undulates from a place, it is inevitable that another air undulates to that place due to the impossibility of vacuum, so the undulation of the other air is the cause of the echo. And you are well aware that this is only valid if the echo occurs from the transmission of the other air to the place of the undulating air that produces the sound, not from the return of the air carrying it due to its striking what resists it in one of the two ways as mentioned above. But it may not be perceived – i.e., the echo – either because of the proximity of the distance between the sound and its reflector, so the sound and the echo are not heard in two distinct times such that the sense is strong enough to perceive their distinctness, so we do not distinguish between them – i.e., between the sound and its echo – due to the sense's inability to distinguish between similar things, so they are perceived as one sound, as in baths and very smooth polished domes; or because the reflector is not hard and smooth, so the returning air due to the resistance of the mentioned reflector (like a ball that is thrown at a soft thing, so its rebound from it is only with weakness), so its return – i.e., the return of the air from that reflector – is weak, so there occurs only a weak, hidden echo that is difficult to perceive. This is if the existence of the resisting reflector is stipulated for echo. But if that is not stipulated, as necessitated by the statement of the Imam, then it is said as he mentioned: His statement: (i.e., a form and description) he interpreted 'quality' (kayfiyya) by it so that it includes the length and shortness of the sound so that the precaution from them is valid by his statement: 'distinguishing in what is heard.' And know that the 'letter' (harf) being that quality (kayfiyya) accidental to the sound is only according to al-Shaykh, and according to a group of investigators: the letter is the sound that possesses that quality. And the more similar to the truth is that it is the sum of the accident and the substance, as stated by some, and the commentator will indicate it later. The echo may not be heard either because of the closeness of the two times as mentioned, or because of its dispersion as in the desert. (And therefore) i.e., because of what we mentioned about the state of echo, the voice of a singer in the desert is weaker in roofed places, because the cause in this is only that the echo accompanies the sound in the roofed place, so his sound then becomes stronger and multiplies due to the echo perceived with it in one time, contrary to the desert where the echo disperses or does not exist according to the opinion stipulating the reflector.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage defines the cause of echo (صدى) and discusses why it may not be heard. The author begins by stating the apparent view: echo is caused by a new undulation of air, not the return of the original air. He explains that when the undulating air strikes a resisting body, the original undulation is lost, and a new undulation arises from the collision and rebound. This new undulation produces the echo. He then notes that some mistakenly think the original air returns with its undulation, but the evidence shows the echo has the same form as the original sound, supporting the new undulation theory. Next, the author discusses the second branch: the opinion of some, including Imam al-Razi, that every sound has an echo due to the impossibility of vacuum—air must move to fill the void left by the original undulation, causing an echo. The author critiques this, noting it only works if the echo comes from air moving into the void, not from the rebound of the same air. He then lists two reasons why an echo may not be perceived: (1) proximity of the reflector, so the sound and echo are too close in time for the ear to distinguish, as in smooth domes; (2) the reflector is not hard and smooth, so the rebound is weak, producing a faint echo. After a brief digression on the definition of 'letter' (حرف) as a quality of sound, the author returns to echo, adding that in a desert, echo may not be heard due to dispersion or absence of a reflector. He contrasts this with roofed places where echo reinforces the sound, making it louder.

Key Terms
صدى
تموج
هواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think echo is the original air returning with its undulation.
Correction: Echo is actually a new undulation created by the collision of the original wave with a resistant body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause of echo?
A) The return of the original air with its original undulation.
B) A new undulation of air resulting from collision and rebound.
C) The transmission of sound through a vacuum.
D) The reflection of sound from a hard smooth surface.

Key Takeaway
Echo is caused by a new air undulation generated when the original sound wave strikes a resistant body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an echo, do you ever pause to consider that Allah is constantly creating new things—that what seems like a repetition is actually a fresh act of creation? How might this reshape your awareness of His power in everyday sounds?
?
Sanusi argues that the echo is not the same wave returning, but something new. In your life, do you sometimes mistake recurring trials as the same old problems, when in fact each is a new creation from Allah with its own purpose? What would change if you saw each 'echo' as a unique test?
?
The author says the new undulation 'resembles' the first but is distinct. Is there a relationship, habit, or sin in your life that you think is just a repeat of the past, but could actually be a fresh situation from Allah calling for a fresh response? Will you treat it as a new opportunity to turn to Him?

When Echo Is Not Heard: Distance and Reflector Conditions
Sanusi discusses reasons why echo may not be heard and critiques the view that every sound has an echo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasons why an echo may not be perceived, including proximity of the reflector and lack of hardness or smoothness, as well as the view that every sound has an echo.

Core Idea
Echo may be inaudible due to timing, reflector quality, or environment.

After establishing the cause of echo, Sanusi discusses conditions under which an echo may not be heard. He critiques the notion that every sound inevitably produces an echo, and then explains two specific physical factors—proximity and reflector smoothness—that can render an echo imperceptible. He also notes the effect of open spaces like deserts versus enclosed spaces.

Guided Path
1
Read about the claim that every sound has an echo
Begin with the second branch, where Imam al-Razi's argument is presented. Notice the author's critique: the argument only holds under a specific interpretation of echo's cause.
Al-Razi's reasoning relies on the impossibility of vacuum, not on the rebound mechanism.
2
Identify the two reasons echo may not be perceived
Read the section beginning with 'لكن قد لا يحس به'. The author lists two causes: (1) proximity of the reflector, so sound and echo are too close in time; (2) the reflector is not hard and smooth, resulting in a weak rebound.
The first reason uses examples of baths and domes; the second uses the analogy of a ball thrown at a soft thing.
3
Examine the digression on 'letter' (harf)
The author briefly discusses the definition of 'letter' as a quality of sound. Note that this is a side point and the main thread returns to echo afterwards.
This relates to terminology differences among scholars.
4
Understand the desert versus roofed places comparison
Read the concluding part where the author compares a singer's voice in the desert (weaker) to in a roofed place (stronger due to echo reinforcement).
This illustrates how environmental conditions affect echo perception.

النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الرابع: الهواء المتموج الحامل للصوت ( الأول : الظاهر أن الصدى أي سبب الصدى ) تموج هواء جديد لا رجوع الهواء الأول ) وذلك لأن الهواء إذا تموج على الوجه الذي عرفته، بالمصادمة والرجوع هو السبب للصدى الشبيه بالصوت الأول ، وكما أن التموج الأول كان بصدم بعد صدم وسكون بعد سكون كذلك الحال في التموج الثاني الذي كان ابتداؤه عند انتهاء الأول. وقد يظن أن متصفاً الهواء المصادم يرجع بتموجه الأول بعينه فيحمل ذلك الصوت الأول إلى السامع. ألا ترى أنَّ الصدى يكون على صفته وهيئته، هذا وإن كان محتملاً إلا أنَّ الأول هو الظاهر الفرع ( الثاني : قد ظن بعض أنَّ لكل صوت صدى ) قال الإمام الرازي: الأشبه ذلك لأنه إذا تموج هواء عن مكان لا بد أن يتموج إلى ذلك المكان هواء آخر لامتناع الخلاء فيكون تموج الهواء الآخر سبباً للصدى . وأنت خبير بأن هذا إنما يتم إذا كان الصدى حادثاً من انتقال الهواء الآخر إلى مكان الهواء المتموج الحاصل للصوت لا من رجوع الهواء الحامل له بسبب مصادمته لما يقاومه على أحد الوجهين كما مر آنفاً. لكن قد لا يحس به ) أي الصدى (إما لقرب المسافة بين الصوت وعاكسه فلا يسمع الصوت والصدى في زمانين متباينين بحيث يقوى الحس على إدراك تباينهما ( فلا نميز بينهما ) أي بين الصوت وصداه لعجز الحس عن التمييز بين الأمثال فيحس بهما على أنهما صوت واحد كما في الحمامات والقباب الملس الصقيلة جداً ( وإما لأن العاكس لا يكون صلباً أملس فيكون الهواء الراجع بسبب مقاومة العاكس المذكور ( كالكرة ) التي ( ترمى إلى شيء لين فلا يكون نبوها عنه إلا مع ضعف ( فيكون رجوعه ) أي رجوع الصحراء إذ ينتشر هناك الصدى أو لا يوجد فيها على القول باشتراط العاكس.
The third type - the first section - the fourth objective: The undulating air that carries sound. (First: It is apparent that echo, that is, the cause of echo) is the undulation of new air, not the return of the first air. And that is because when the air undulates in the manner you have known, as previously mentioned, until the undulating part of it strikes a body that resists it and sends it back, that undulation which had occurred to it does not remain in the struck air. Rather, due to its striking and returning, an undulation similar to the first undulation occurs in it. So this new undulation resulting from the striking and returning is the cause of the echo that resembles the first sound. And just as the first undulation was by impact after impact and stillness after stillness, so is the case with the second undulation whose beginning was at the end of the first. And one might think that the struck air returns with its first undulation itself and thus carries that first sound to the hearer. Do you not see that the echo is upon its description and form? This, although possible, yet the first is apparent. (The second branch: Some have thought that every sound has an echo.) Imam al-Razi said: The most similar is that, because when air undulates from a place, it is inevitable that another air undulates to that place due to the impossibility of vacuum, so the undulation of the other air is the cause of the echo. And you are well aware that this is only valid if the echo occurs from the transmission of the other air to the place of the undulating air that produces the sound, not from the return of the air carrying it due to its striking what resists it in one of the two ways as mentioned above. But it may not be perceived – i.e., the echo – either because of the proximity of the distance between the sound and its reflector, so the sound and the echo are not heard in two distinct times such that the sense is strong enough to perceive their distinctness, so we do not distinguish between them – i.e., between the sound and its echo – due to the sense's inability to distinguish between similar things, so they are perceived as one sound, as in baths and very smooth polished domes; or because the reflector is not hard and smooth, so the returning air due to the resistance of the mentioned reflector (like a ball that is thrown at a soft thing, so its rebound from it is only with weakness), so its return – i.e., the return of the air from that reflector – is weak, so there occurs only a weak, hidden echo that is difficult to perceive. This is if the existence of the resisting reflector is stipulated for echo. But if that is not stipulated, as necessitated by the statement of the Imam, then it is said as he mentioned: His statement: (i.e., a form and description) he interpreted 'quality' (kayfiyya) by it so that it includes the length and shortness of the sound so that the precaution from them is valid by his statement: 'distinguishing in what is heard.' And know that the 'letter' (harf) being that quality (kayfiyya) accidental to the sound is only according to al-Shaykh, and according to a group of investigators: the letter is the sound that possesses that quality. And the more similar to the truth is that it is the sum of the accident and the substance, as stated by some, and the commentator will indicate it later. The echo may not be heard either because of the closeness of the two times as mentioned, or because of its dispersion as in the desert. (And therefore) i.e., because of what we mentioned about the state of echo, the voice of a singer in the desert is weaker in roofed places, because the cause in this is only that the echo accompanies the sound in the roofed place, so his sound then becomes stronger and multiplies due to the echo perceived with it in one time, contrary to the desert where the echo disperses or does not exist according to the opinion stipulating the reflector.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage defines the cause of echo (صدى) and discusses why it may not be heard. The author begins by stating the apparent view: echo is caused by a new undulation of air, not the return of the original air. He explains that when the undulating air strikes a resisting body, the original undulation is lost, and a new undulation arises from the collision and rebound. This new undulation produces the echo. He then notes that some mistakenly think the original air returns with its undulation, but the evidence shows the echo has the same form as the original sound, supporting the new undulation theory. Next, the author discusses the second branch: the opinion of some, including Imam al-Razi, that every sound has an echo due to the impossibility of vacuum—air must move to fill the void left by the original undulation, causing an echo. The author critiques this, noting it only works if the echo comes from air moving into the void, not from the rebound of the same air. He then lists two reasons why an echo may not be perceived: (1) proximity of the reflector, so the sound and echo are too close in time for the ear to distinguish, as in smooth domes; (2) the reflector is not hard and smooth, so the rebound is weak, producing a faint echo. After a brief digression on the definition of 'letter' (حرف) as a quality of sound, the author returns to echo, adding that in a desert, echo may not be heard due to dispersion or absence of a reflector. He contrasts this with roofed places where echo reinforces the sound, making it louder.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think every sound inevitably has an audible echo.
Correction: An echo may not be heard if the reflector is too close or if it is not hard and smooth.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a reason why echo may not be heard?
A) The sound is too loud.
B) The reflector is too far away.
C) The reflector is not hard and smooth.
D) The air is still.

Key Takeaway
Echo may not be perceived due to proximity of the reflector, lack of hardness/smoothness, or dispersion in open spaces.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak truth, do you expect immediate affirmation (an echo) from others? The lesson shows that echo may be imperceptible due to conditions beyond your control. Are you willing to speak truth even when you may not hear a return, trusting that its impact is real but hidden?
?
The author notes that a soft reflector produces a weak echo. Have you ever been a 'soft reflector'—absorbing divine guidance without reflecting it back into your actions? What concrete step will you take today to become a hard, smooth reflector so that God's words resound clearly through your life?

Why Echo May Not Be Heard
Passage explains reasons echo may not be heard and compares enclosed vs. open spaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why echo may not be heard and how enclosed spaces amplify sound compared to open spaces.

Core Idea
Echo depends on reflection and space; it can be absent due to timing or dispersion.

This passage from al-Iji's Mawaqif explains the acoustic phenomenon of echo. It identifies two causes for echo not being heard: the closeness of the original and reflected sounds, or the spreading of sound in open areas. The author then applies this to explain why a singer's voice is stronger in a covered building than in a desert. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'إما... وإما' for the two reasons.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage gives two reasons why echo may not be heard: closeness of the times and spreading. Find these in the text and note how the author connects them.
The first reason references a previous discussion about the two times.
3
Compare enclosed and open spaces
See how the author uses the example of a singer to illustrate the effect. Ask: Why is the voice stronger in a covered building? How does echo behave differently in the desert?
This shows the practical implication of the acoustic principles.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الرابع : الهواء المتموج الحامل للصوت منه
قد لا يسمع الصدى إما لقرب الزمانين كيما مر وإما لانتشاره كما في الصحراء ( ولذلك ) أي ولما ذكرناه من حال الصدى ) كان صوت المغني في الصحراء أضعف في المسقفات إذ ليس السبب في هذا إلا أنَّ الصدى يقترن بالصوت في المسقف فيتقوى ويتضاعف صوته حينئذ بالصدى المحسوس معه في زمان واحد بخلاف الصحراء إذ ينتشر هناك الصدى أو لا يوجد فيها على القول باشتراط العاكس.
The third type - the first section: the fourth intent: the undulating air that carries sound from it. Echo may not be heard either due to the closeness of the two times as previously mentioned, or due to its spreading as in the desert. (And therefore) i.e., because of what we mentioned about the state of echo, the voice of a singer in the desert is weaker in covered buildings, for the cause of this is only that echo joins with the sound in the covered building, so his voice then strengthens and multiplies with the perceived echo with it in one time, unlike the desert, for echo spreads there or does not exist there according to the view requiring a reflector.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of echo and why it may not be heard. It states two reasons: either the closeness of the two times (the original sound and the reflected sound are too close together, as previously discussed) or the spreading out of the sound in an open area like a desert. It then applies this to the example of a singer: in a covered building (musaqqafat), the echo merges with the original sound, strengthening and multiplying it, so the voice is louder. In the desert, the echo either spreads out and dissipates or does not occur at all if one holds the view that a reflecting surface is necessary. Thus, the passage contrasts the acoustic effects of enclosed versus open spaces.

Misconception: Echo always occurs in any environment.
Correction: The passage shows that echo may not be heard due to timing or space; it requires a reflective surface and proper conditions.

D) The interference of other sounds and the absorption by surfaces

Key Takeaway
Echo may not be heard due to timing or dispersion; enclosed spaces amplify sound by merging echo with the original.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If your good deeds feel like a sound lost in a desert, could it be because you spread yourself too thin across many distractions instead of concentrating in a 'covered space' of consistency? What one area of worship will you contain—give undivided attention—this week?

Permissibility of Combining Two Sukun Letters
Classifies the permissibility of combining two sukun letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of combining two sukun letters in Arabic and the scholarly agreement and disagreement on each.

Core Idea
Not all sukun combinations are forbidden; the type determines the rule.

When reciting Arabic, we sometimes encounter two letters without vowels. Scholars have debated whether this is allowed. This passage from al-Mawaqif clarifies the classification: some combinations are unanimously allowed, some are disputed, and others are impossible. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
The passage begins by asking whether two sukun letters can be adjacent. This is the fourth objective of the third type.
Sukun means a letter without a vowel, carrying a sukūn sign.
2
Identify the first case
The first case is an assimilated consonant (mudgham) preceded by a vowel. This combination is unanimously allowed. Example from the Quran: 'wa la al-dalleen'.
Assimilation means the first consonant is merged into the second.
3
Examine the second case
The second case covers two consonants or a non-assimilated consonant preceded by a vowel. Some scholars allow it (e.g., in pausing on 'Zayd'), while others claim a hidden vowel, so no real combination.
This is a point of ikhtilāf (disagreement).
4
Note the third case
The third case is combining two vowel letters (i.e., two long vowels) or a consonant followed by a vowel. This is unanimously impossible because vowels cannot be sukūn.
This ends the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - القسم الثاني : المقصد الرابع: هل يمكن الجمع بين الساكنين ؟ أما صامت مدغم في مثله قبله مصوت نحو ولا الضالين فجائز جمعهما اتفاقاً وأما الصامتان أو صامت غير مدغم قبله مصوت فجوزه قوم كما في الوقف على الثلاثي الساكن الأوسط كزيد وعمرو بل جوزوا أيضاً جمع ساكنين صامتين قبلهما مصوت فيجتمع حينئذ ثلاثة سواكن كما يقال في الفارسية كارد وكوشت ومنهم من منعه وجعل ثمة أي فيما ذكرنا من الصور حركة مختلسة خفية جداً. فلا يحس بها على ما ينبغي فيظن أنه اجتمع هناك ساكنان أو أكثر. وأما اجتماع ساكنين مصوتين أو صامت بعده مصوت فلا نزاع في امتناعه
The third type - the second section: the fourth objective: Is it possible to combine two quiescent (sakin) letters? As for a consonant assimilated (mudgham) into its like preceded by a vowel, such as 'wa la al-dalleen', then combining them is permissible unanimously. As for two consonants, or a non-assimilated consonant preceded by a vowel, some people permitted their combination, as in pausing on a triliteral word with a middle sukun like Zayd and 'Amr. Rather, they also permitted combining two quiescent consonants preceded by a vowel, so three sukun letters then combine, as is said in Persian: 'kard' and 'kusht'. And among them are those who prohibited it and made there – i.e., in the images we mentioned – a very subtle stolen vowel movement. Thus it is not sensed as it should be, so one thinks that two or more sukun letters have combined there. As for the combination of two vowel letters, or a consonant followed by a vowel, there is no dispute about its impossibility.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage classifies the possibility of combining two quiescent (sakin) letters in Arabic. It first states the question: can two sukun letters be combined? Then it gives the first case: a consonant that is assimilated (mudgham) into a like consonant preceded by a vowel – this is permissible unanimously, as seen in the phrase 'wa la al-dalleen'. Next, it discusses two consonants or a non-assimilated consonant preceded by a vowel: some scholars allow their combination, for example in pausing on a triliteral word like 'Zayd' or 'Amr' where the middle letter is sukun. Some even allow three sukun letters, citing Persian words. Others deny this and claim a very subtle hidden vowel movement, making it seem like two or more sukun letters are adjacent. Finally, it states that combining two vowel letters or a consonant followed by a vowel is impossible by consensus. The passage thus distinguishes between different types of sukun sequences and the rulings on them.

Key Takeaway

Why Derived Words Are Three-Letter Roots
Explains the reason for three-letter roots and prohibition of stopping on a voweled letter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the phonological principle that requires derived Arabic words to have three-letter roots, based on the avoidance of repulsion between a voweled first letter and a quiescent last letter.

Core Idea
Three-letter roots exist to prevent the repulsion between a voweled letter and a quiescent letter.

Imam al-Razi presents a fascinating phonological explanation for a core feature of Arabic morphology: why derived words are typically three-letter roots. He argues that this structure arises from the need to avoid a dissonance between the initially voweled letter and the final quiescent letter in pause, with a medial consonant resolving the tension. This passage will deepen your appreciation for the systematic nature of Arabic word formation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, paying attention to the key terms like وقف, متحرك, ساكن, منافرة. Then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
Notice how the passage begins with 'ولذلك أيضاً' connecting it to a previous discussion.
2
Identify the problem
The passage identifies a repulsion (منافرة) between the first moved letter and the last quiescent letter. Why is this problematic? Consider the sound quality.
The text says the first letter is necessarily moved, the last is sukun in pause.
3
Understand the solution
The solution is to insert a third letter that is medial, which can be either moved or quiescent, thus balancing the word. Why does this work? The medial letter does not cause repulsion with either end.
Also note the additional point about initial movement being heavier.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this principle shapes Arabic morphology. Why are most Arabic roots three-letter? This passage gives a phonetic rationale.
The common misconception is that three-letter roots are arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي: قوله: (ولذلك أيضاً لم يجوزوا الوقف على المتحرك ولذا كان الأصل عندهم في كل كلمة متصرفة أن تكون عن ثلاثة أحرف. لأنَّ الحرف الأول متحرك البتة لما عرفت، والأخير ساكن في الوقف وبينهما منافرة فكرهوا مقارنتهما وفصلوا بينهما بثالث ليحصل الاعتدال، وذلك الثالث لا يحتاج إلى معدل آخر لأنه لما جاز عليه الحركة والسكون من حيث هو متوسط لم تتحقق المنافرة بينه وبين شيء من طرفيه وأيضاً الحركة الابتدائية أثقل من الحركة المتوسطة فالتنافر بين السكون وبينها أشد منه بينه وبين المتوسطة، ويؤكده أنه إذا حصل النطق بحرفين متحركين حصل ضرب من الملال فيستلذ بالسكون فوق ما يستلذ به إذا كان النطق بالحركة الواحدة فقط . كذا في شرح الملخص قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي (الخ هذا الكلام وكذا أكثر ما ذكر في مباحث المسموعات منقول من الملخص وشرحه .
Imam al-Razi said: His saying: (And therefore also they did not permit stopping on the moved letter, and so the principle for them in every derived word was that it should consist of three letters. Because the first letter is necessarily moved, as you know, and the last is quiescent in pausing, and between them there is repulsion; so they disliked bringing them together and separated them with a third to achieve balance. And that third does not need another modifier because since movement and quiescence are possible for it inasmuch as it is medial, repulsion is not actualized between it and either of its ends. Also, the initial movement is heavier than the medial movement, so the repulsion between quiescence and it is more severe than between it and the medial movement. And it is confirmed that when speech occurs with two moved letters, a kind of fatigue arises, and one enjoys the quiescence more than one enjoys it when speech is with only one movement. Thus in the commentary on al-Mulakhkhas. His saying: (Imam al-Razi said, etc.) this speech and most of what was mentioned in the investigations of audible matters are transmitted from al-Mulakhkhas and its commentary.)
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Imam al-Razi explaining why the Arabs did not allow stopping on a moved (vowelled) letter. The reason is that the first letter of a derived word is necessarily moved, the last is sukun in pause, and the repulsion between them is strong. To avoid this, they inserted a third letter to create balance, and that third letter, being medial, can accept both movement and sukun, thus avoiding repulsion. Additionally, initial movement is heavier than medial movement, so the repulsion is greater. This is supported by the observation that speaking two moved letters causes fatigue, while the enjoyment of sukun is greater after a single movement. The editorial note states that this discussion and most of the material on audible matters is taken from al-Mulakhkhas and its commentary.

متحرك
ساكن
منافرة
ثلاثي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That three-letter roots are an arbitrary convention of Arabic morphology.
Correction: This passage shows that three-letter roots are based on a phonological principle: to avoid the repulsion between a voweled initial and a quiescent final, a medial consonant creates balance.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The three-letter root structure in Arabic derived words is a phonological solution to the inherent repulsion between a voweled first letter and a quiescent final letter in pause.

Two Proofs That Movements Are Parts of Vowels
Two proofs that movements are parts of vowels, plus a note on vowel width.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments demonstrating that vocalic movements (harakat) are incomplete parts of vowels (musawwatat), and why this matters for Arabic phonetics.

Core Idea
Vocalic movements are the minimal extremes of vowels, proven by logic and sensory extension.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents two concise proofs that link phonological intuition to rational analysis. The first uses the concept of extremes in quantity, while the second appeals to direct sensory experience of extending movements into full vowels. Understanding these arguments clarifies the foundational relationship between short vowels and long vowels in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Get a sense of the two-part structure.
Notice the words 'أما أولاً' and 'وأما ثانياً' which clearly mark the two proofs.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the first proof (based on increase/decrease and extremes) and the second proof (based on extension of movements). Understand each premise and conclusion.
The first proof uses induction (الاستقراء); the second uses sensory evidence (الحس).
3
Reflect on the concluding observation
Read the final sentence about the width of vowels (alif, ya, waw) and the heaviness of dammah. Note how this relates to the physical articulation of vowels.
This observation is not a proof but an additional note about the hierarchy of vowels.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركات أبعاض المصوتات. أما أولاً : فلان هذه المصوتات قابلة للزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك فله طرفان ولا طرف في النقصان للمصوتات إلا هذه الحركات بشهادة الاستقراء وأما ثانياً: فلان الحركات لو لم تكن أبعاض المصوتات لما حصلت المصوتات بتمديدها فإنَّ الحركة إذا كانت مخالفة لها ومددتها لم يمكنك أن تذكر المصوت إلا باستئناف صامت آخر يجعل المصوت تبعاً له لكن الحس شاهد بحصول المصوتات بمجرد تمديد الحركات . ثم إِنَّ أوسع المصوتات باعتبار انفتاح الفم هو الألف ثم الياء ثم الواو وأثقلها الضمة المحتاجة إلى مزيد تحريك الشفتين ثم
The movements are parts of the vowels. As for first: because these vowels are subject to increase and decrease, and everything that is so has two extremes, and there is no extreme in the decrease of the vowels except these movements, by the testimony of induction. And as for second: because if the movements were not parts of the vowels, then the vowels would not be obtained by extending them. For if the movement were different from them and you extended it, you could not produce the vowel except by starting another consonant that makes the vowel dependent on it. But the sense bears witness that the vowels are obtained by merely extending the movements. Then the widest of the vowels in consideration of the opening of the mouth is the alif, then the ya, then the waw, and the heaviest of them is the dammah, which needs more moving of the lips. Then
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs that vocalic movements (harakat) are parts (ab'ād) of vowels (musawwatāt). The first proof is based on increase and decrease: vowels can be lengthened or shortened; anything with increase and decrease has extremes, and the only extreme on the side of decrease is these movements, as shown by induction. The second proof is sensory: if movements were not parts of vowels, extending them would not produce vowels; but in fact, when we extend a movement, we hear a vowel without needing another consonant. Then it notes the relative width of vowels: the most open is alif, then ya, then waw, and the heaviest is the dammah, which requires more lip movement.

Correction: This passage shows that movements are actually incomplete parts of vowels; they are continuous, not separate.
