Clarifying the Meaning of 'Deep-Penetrating'
Clarification of the term 'deep-penetrating' as evidence of small separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the clarification of the term 'deep-penetrating' (ghā'iṣ) and why it indicates small separation.

Core Idea
Depth of penetration proves smallness of gaps.

The second gloss addresses the phrase 'but it is deep-penetrating'. The commentator suggests a more appropriate interpretation: that the depth itself is proof of the smallness of the separation. This indicates that the heat's penetration is not a correction but an evidence.

Guided Path

Read the gloss carefully

2
Understand the interpretation
The depth is a proof (دليل) that the separation (تفريق) is small. This is the preferred reading.
This comes from the context of the main commentary.
3
Note the nature of the gloss
The glossator says this is not a place for correction (استدراك) but a clarification.
The phrase 'as is not hidden from the one who tastes' indicates intuitive understanding.
4
Reflect on the significance

Definition and Cause of Saltiness
Defines saltiness as intermediate and explains its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how saltiness is defined as an intermediate quality and how it is generated from a balanced mixture of bitter and pungent elements.

Core Idea
Saltiness is not a simple taste but a balance between bitterness and pungency.

This passage examines the nature of saltiness, showing that it is not a fundamental taste but an intermediate one. It explains why salty things can sometimes taste bitter and sometimes pungent, and it provides both a rational verification and a practical cause for this phenomenon. As you read, notice how the author uses both empirical example and theoretical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of saltiness
Read the Arabic and English to see how saltiness is defined as intermediate between bitterness and pungency.
Pay attention to the words 'تميل' and 'مرة'.
2
Identify the verification method
The author gives a practical experiment: taking bitter ashes, mixing with water, and cooking to produce saltiness. Consider why this demonstrates intermediate nature.
This is a 'تحقيق'.
3
Understand the causal theory
The passage states that saltiness arises from a balanced mixture (مخالطة باعتدال) of tasteless water and dry, burnt earthy parts. Note how imbalance leads to bitterness.
4
Consider practical implications
The passage mentions making salt from ashes, alkali, etc. Relate this to the causal theory.
This connects theory to everyday experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولذلك ) أي ولأن الملوحة كيفية متوسطة بين كيفيتي المرارة والحرافة ( تميل) الملوحة ( إلى المرارة مرة وإلى الحرافة (أخرى أي يكون طعم المالح تارة قريباً من المرارة بحيث يتوهم أنه مر وتارة قريباً من الحرافة بحيث يتخيل أنه حريف وتحقيقه) أي تحقيق كون الملوحة متوسطة بينهما ( أنه إذا أخذ لطيف الرماد المر وخلط بالماء وطبخ حصلت الملوحة ) . وهذا ما قيل من أنّ سبب حدوث الملوحة مخالطة رطوبة مائية قليلة الطعم أو عديمته بأجزاء أرضية محترقة يابسة المزاج مرة الطعم مخالطة باعتدال فإنَّ الأجزاء الأرضية إذا كثرت أمرت ومن هذا السبب تتولد الأملاح وتصير المياه ملحاً وقد يصنع الملح من الرماد والقلي والنورة وغير ذلك بأن يطبخ في الماء ويصفى ويغلى ذلك الماء حتى ينعقد ملحا أو يترك حتى ينعقد بنفسه
(Therefore) and because saltiness is a quality intermediate between the two qualities of bitterness and pungency, (saltiness inclines) to bitterness sometimes and to pungency (another) i.e., the taste of salty is sometimes close to bitterness so that it is imagined to be bitter, and sometimes close to pungency so that it is imagined to be pungent. And its verification i.e., the verification that saltiness is intermediate between them (is that if the fine part of bitter ashes is taken and mixed with water and cooked, saltiness results). And this is what is said: that the cause of the occurrence of saltiness is the mixing of a watery moisture of little or no taste with earthy burnt parts of dry temperament and bitter taste, in a balanced mixture; for if the earthy parts increase, they become bitter. From this cause salts are generated and waters become salt. And salt can be made from ashes, alkali, lime, and other things by cooking them in water, filtering, and boiling that water until it solidifies into salt, or leaving it until it solidifies by itself.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines saltiness and explains its cause. It begins by stating that saltiness is a quality (kayfiyya) intermediate between bitterness (al-marāra) and pungency (al-ḥarāfa). The author supports this by noting that the salty taste sometimes resembles bitterness and sometimes pungency, so it lies between them. The verification of this intermediate nature is given: taking the fine part of bitter ashes, mixing with water, and cooking produces saltiness. Then the cause of saltiness is explained: a balanced mixture (mukhālaṭa bi-‘tiddāl) of a watery moisture of little or no taste with burnt earthy parts that are dry and bitter. If the earthy parts dominate, bitterness results. From this process salts are generated and waters become salty. The passage also describes practical salt-making from ashes, alkali, and lime by cooking and evaporation.

Objection: Sourness Caused by Heat? The Real Agent is Cold
Objection and answer on the cause of sourness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that weak heat causes sourness is resolved by affirming that non-intense cold is the true agent.

Core Idea
Heat only enables cold to act; it does not directly produce sourness.

This passage presents a sharp objection and its answer. Someone argues that sourness in juice or milk is clearly caused by weak heat, not cold. The response reinterprets the observation: heat merely breaks the intensity of cold, allowing a mild cold to produce sourness. Understanding this requires distinguishing the ultimate cause from the immediate trigger.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that sourness in juice and milk is produced by weak heat, not by cold. Consider why this seems plausible from everyday experience.
The objection is based on direct observation: heat is applied, sourness results.
2
Study the answer's logic
The answer denies that heat is the agent. Instead, it explains that intense cold is broken by weak heat, resulting in non-intense cold which then causes sourness.
Notice the shift: heat is only a catalyst, not the efficient cause.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
The answer preserves the principle that cold produces sourness while explaining away the apparent counterexample. Heat merely modifies the intensity of cold.
This shows how apparent counterexamples can be explained without rejecting the original doctrine.

When intense cold is present, weak heat can break its intensity, resulting in a non-intense cold that then causes sourness. Thus, the ultimate cause remains cold, not heat.

Cold's Tastes: Astringency, Sourness, and Constriction
Classification of cold's tastes in three substrates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how cold produces different tastes (astringency, sourness, constriction) depending on the density or subtlety of the substrate.

Core Idea
Cold's tastes vary by substrate: dense gives astringency, subtle gives sourness, moderate gives constriction.

This passage classifies the effects of cold on different substrates, explaining how the same quality (cold) yields distinct tastes due to variations in condensation and resistance. The author details three cases: dense, subtle, and moderate substrates, each producing a characteristic taste with a specific degree of aversion. Understanding this classification helps explain natural phenomena like fruit ripening.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument. Note the three substrates and the tastes they produce.
2
Identify the three substrates and tastes
The passage discusses dense (kathīf), subtle (laṭīf), and moderate (muʿtadil) substrates. List the taste each produces: astringency, sourness, constriction.
Astringency approaches bitterness in aversion.
3
Understand the mechanism
Cold causes condensation. In dense substrates, resistance doubles condensation, causing strong astringency. In subtle, condensation is weaker, yielding sourness. Moderate gives intermediate constriction.
The fruit example shows how astringent fruit becomes sour as it ripens (becomes more watery).
4
Analyze the aversion hierarchy
The passage ranks the tastes by aversion: astringency is most aversive, then constriction, then sourness. Notice why the author says astringent fruit grips both inside and outside of the tongue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبارد يفعل كالحار كيفية غير ملائمة إذ من شأنه التكثيف الذي لا يلائم الأجسام أيضاً . لكن عدم ملاءمته أقل من عدم ملاءمة التفريق ولذلك كانت الكيفيات الحادثة بواسطة التفريق أشد في المنافرة من الكيفيات الحادثة بتوسط التكثيف . ثم إنَّ هذه الكيفيات أيضاً مختلفة في عدم الملاءمة على حسب مراتب التكثيف في القوة والضعف، وإليه الإشارة بقوله : ( ففي الكثيف) أي فيفعل البارد في القابل الكثيف عفوصة لأنه يتضاعف التكثيف يعني أن الكثيف يمنع البرودة عن النفوذ ويقاومها فيجتمع حينئذ أجزاء البرودة، ويؤثر فيه تأثيراً عظيماً، ويكثفه تكثيفاً بليغاً متضاعفاً، فيحدث فيه العفوصة التي تقرب من المرارة في المنافرة، (و) يفعل البارد (في) القابل اللطيف حموضة لأنَّ اللطيف لا يقاوم البرودة فينفذ في أعماقه ويكثفه تكثيفاً أقل بكثير مما في القابل الكثيف، فيحدث فيه كيفية يكون عدم ملاءمتها أقل من عدم ملاءمة العفوصة بكثير أيضاً ، وهي الحموضة وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار بقوله: ) لأنه ( أي الفاعل البارد ( يكثف ( القابل اللطيف ( يبرده ويغوص ) فيه ( بلطافته ) أي لطافته فيضعف فيه تأثيره ) فيكون عدم ملاءمته ) أي عدم ملاءمة الطعم الحادث في ذلك القابل اللطيف (بين بين . ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ الصواب تبديلهما بأقل كما أشرنا إليه (ولذلك ) أي : ولأن الحموضة تحدث من فعل البارد في اللطيف ( كان الثمر (العفص لشدة برده وكثافته ) كلما ازداد مائية ) ولطافة واعتدل قليلا بإسخان الشمس المنضج ) ازداد حموضة و يفعل البارد (في) القابل ( المعتدل قبضاً وهو في عدم الملاءمة ( دون العفوصة) وفوق الحموضة لأنّ تكثيف البرودة في المعتدل أقل من تكثيفها في الكثيف، وأكثر من تكثيفها في اللطيف على قياس ما مر فيحدث فيه كيفية عدم ملاءمتها بين بين وهو القبض وكونه في عدم الملاءمة فوق الحموضة ظاهر، وأما كونه في ذلك دون العفوصة فإليه أشار بقوله : ( إذ العفص يقبض باطن اللسان وظاهره )
And the cold acts like the hot in producing a non-pleasant quality, since its property is condensation which is also not compatible with bodies. But its incompatibility is less than the incompatibility of separation. Therefore the qualities produced by means of separation are more intense in aversion than qualities produced by means of condensation. Then these qualities also differ in incompatibility according to the degrees of condensation in strength and weakness, and to this he pointed by his saying: 'So in the dense' i.e., the cold acts in the dense receptive subject producing astringency because condensation is doubled, meaning that the dense prevents the cold from penetrating and resists it, so then the parts of cold gather, affecting it greatly, and condenses it with a strong doubled condensation, thus producing astringency which approaches bitterness in aversion. And the cold acts in the subtle receptive subject producing sourness because the subtle does not resist the cold, so it penetrates into its depths and condenses it with a condensation much less than in the dense receptive subject, thus producing a quality whose incompatibility is much less than the incompatibility of astringency, which is sourness. And to what we mentioned he pointed by his saying: 'Because' i.e., the cold agent 'condenses' the subtle receptive subject 'cools it and penetrates' into it 'by its subtlety' i.e., its subtlety, so its effect weakens in it, so its incompatibility i.e., the incompatibility of the taste occurring in that subtle receptive subject 'is intermediate'. And it is not hidden from you that the correct thing is to replace them with 'less' as we indicated. 'Therefore' i.e., because sourness occurs from the action of cold in the subtle, 'the astringent fruit' due to its intense cold and density, 'every time it increases in wateriness' and subtlety and becomes moderate a little by the heating of the ripening sun, 'it increases in sourness'. And the cold acts in the moderate receptive subject producing constriction, and it is in incompatibility below astringency and above sourness because the condensation of cold in the moderate is less than its condensation in the dense and greater than its condensation in the subtle, in analogy to what passed, so a quality arises whose incompatibility is intermediate, which is constriction. And its being above sourness in incompatibility is apparent, and as for its being below astringency in that, he pointed to it by his saying: 'Since the astringent (fruit) grips the inside and outside of the tongue' together, so nature strongly recoils from it, 'and the constrictor grips only its outside, so the recoil is not at that extreme.'
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage classifies the effects of cold in producing tastes. It first states that cold, like heat, produces a non-pleasant quality (kayfiyya ghayr mulāʾima) because its property is condensation (takhthīf), which is also incompatible with bodies. However, cold's incompatibility is less than that caused by separation (tafrīq). Therefore, tastes produced by separation are more aversive than those from condensation. Then it explains how the degree of aversion varies according to the intensity of condensation. The author then details three cases using the commentator's phrasing: (1) In a dense substrate (al-kathīf), cold produces astringency (ʿufūṣa) because the substrate resists cold, causing a doubling of condensation, resulting in astringency close to bitterness. (2) In a subtle substrate (al-laṭīf), cold produces sourness (ḥumūḍa) because the subtle does not resist, so condensation is less, resulting in a less aversive taste. (3) In a moderate substrate (al-muʿtadil), cold produces constriction (qabḍ), which is intermediate in aversion. The fruit example: astringent fruit (al-ʿafṣ) becomes more sour as it becomes more watery and moderate from sun ripening. The passage concludes by clarifying that astringent fruit grips both inside and outside of the tongue, causing strong recoil, while constrictor fruit grips only the outside, so the recoil is less extreme.

Moderate Temperature and Pleasant Taste
States that moderate temperature produces a pleasant taste by avoiding extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why moderate temperature produces a pleasant taste and why it is not simply due to weak heat.

Core Idea
Pleasant taste arises from balance, not from weak extremes.

In this passage, the author explains the effect of the moderate temperature (al-muʿtadil) that lies between hot and cold. He clarifies that its pleasant result comes from avoiding both excessive separation and excessive condensation, acting in a balanced manner. This corrects a common misconception that such a taste is merely from weak heat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's claim and reasoning sink in.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the negative statements (does not separate strongly, does not condense strongly).
2
Identify the two extremes avoided
The moderate avoids strong separation (tafrīq shadeed) and strong condensation (takhthīf qawī). Contrast with intense heat and cold.
These are the actions of extreme temperatures.
3
Understand the balanced action
Instead of extremes, the moderate does a 'between between' action (fiʿl bayn bayn). This balanced action produces the pleasant taste.
4
Note the correction of misconception
Some think the pleasant taste comes from weak heat, but the author says it is from the moderate itself. Recognize the distinction.
Weak heat would still be a form of heat, not balance.
5
Reflect on the principle of balance
Consider why balance (iʿtidāl) is often associated with pleasantness in many contexts. This passage illustrates a broader philosophical idea.

This passage explains the effect of the moderate temperature (al-muʿtadil) between hot and cold. It states that the moderate produces a pleasant (mulāʾim) action and taste. The reason is that it does not cause strong separation (tafrīq) like intense heat, nor strong condensation (takhthīf) like intense cold. Instead, it acts in a balanced way (fiʿl bayn bayn). The resulting taste is therefore agreeable. The passage also notes a common misconception: some think this pleasant taste comes from weak heat, but the author corrects this—it is from the moderate, not from weak heat.

Reconciling Ibn Sina on Sourness
Reconciles Ibn Sina's contradictory statements on sourness causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how apparent contradictions in Ibn Sina's writings are resolved by distinguishing essential from accidental causation.

Core Idea
Heat causes sourness accidentally; cold causes it essentially.

Ibn Sina appears to contradict himself about whether heat or cold makes things sour. This lesson shows how a later scholar resolved the problem: heat triggers a process, but cold is the real cause. Understanding this distinction is key to appreciating how classical thinkers harmonized seemingly conflicting texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the clear statement of contradiction and the answer that follows.
Pay attention to the terms 'bi-l-dhat' (essentially) and 'bi-l-ʿaraḍ' (accidentally).
2
Identify the contradiction
Ibn Sina says heat causes sourness in one place, and cold in another. Why would these both be considered correct?
3
Understand the answer: essential vs accidental
The reconciler says heat is an accidental cause, not an essential one. That means heat sets the stage, but cold is the direct agent. This distinction resolves the contradiction.
Essential cause: what directly produces the effect. Accidental cause: something that indirectly leads to it.
4
Follow the verification process
The passage explains how sweet phlegm becomes sour: foreign heat causes boiling, which disperses innate heat, then cold dominates and produces sourness. Trace each step.
This shows heat as trigger, cold as enforcer.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why this explanation is satisfying. It preserves the truth of both statements by clarifying their different causal roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ الشيخ في مباحث الأخلاط من القانون جعل فاعل الحموضة الحرارة وفي موضع آخر البرودة فبينهما تناقض، وأجاب عنه بعض الفضلاء بأنَّ الشيخ وإن جعل الحرارة في بحث الأخلاط فاعل الحموضة في البلغم الحامض لكنها فاعلة لها أو لا وبالذات بل بالعرض فلا يناقض كون البرودة فاعلة لها أو لا وبالذات توحقيقه أنَّ البلغم الحلو كما يصير حامضاً لمخالطة شيء غريب به. وهو السواداء الحامض فقد يصير أيضاً حامضاً بسبب أمر يحدث في نفسه وهو أن يعرض البلغم الحلو ما يعرض لسائر العصارات من الغليان أو لا، بسبب الحرارة الغريبة ثم التحمض ثانياً بسبب الحرارة الغريبة ثم التحمض ثانيا بسبب استيلاء البرد فالحرارة الغريبة موجبة للغليان وتحليل الحرارة الغريزية وجذبها إلى نفسها بسبب المجانسة ثم البرد المستولي الأسباب يوجب الحموضة فتكون الحرارة فاعلة للحموضة بالعرض لا أو لا وبالذات من وبهذا استولى البرد الخارجي على البلغم الحلو بحمضه
And know that the Sheikh (Ibn Sina) in the discussions of humors in the Canon made heat the agent of sourness, and in another place made cold the agent, so there is a contradiction between them. And some virtuous one answered for him that although the Sheikh in the discussion of humors made heat the agent of sourness in sour phlegm, yet it is an agent of it not essentially and primarily but accidentally, so it does not contradict that cold is the agent essentially and primarily. Its verification is that sweet phlegm, just as it becomes sour by mixing with something foreign to it, which is black bile sour, may also become sour due to something occurring in itself, namely that sweet phlegm undergoes what happens to other fluids from boiling or not, due to foreign heat, then secondarily souring due to foreign heat, then secondarily souring due to the domination of cold. So foreign heat causes boiling and dissolves the innate heat and attracts it to itself due to similarity, then the cold that dominates the causes produces sourness. Thus heat is an agent of sourness accidentally, not primarily and essentially. By this, the external cold dominates the sweet phlegm and makes it sour.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage addresses an apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's Canon of Medicine. In one place, Ibn Sina says heat causes sourness; in another, cold does. The reconciler explains that heat causes sourness accidentally (bi-l-ʿaraḍ), not essentially (bi-l-dhāt). The essential cause is cold. The explanation: Sweet phlegm can become sour either by mixing with sour black bile, or by an internal process: foreign heat causes boiling and disperses innate heat, then cold takes over and produces sourness. Thus heat is only indirectly (by triggering the process) while cold is the direct agent. This resolves the contradiction.

الشيخ
القانون
الحرارة الغريبة
البرودة

بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina contradicted himself about the cause of sourness.
Correction: Ibn Sina did not contradict himself; heat is an accidental cause (trigger), while cold is the essential (direct) cause.

Checkpoint
According to the reconciler, how is heat related to sourness?
A) Heat is the essential cause
B) Heat is the accidental cause
C) Heat has no role
D) Heat and cold both cause sourness equally

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's statements on sourness are reconciled by distinguishing essential (cold) and accidental (heat) causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter apparent contradictions in your understanding of the world or your faith, do you tend to dismiss one side or seek a deeper reconciliation like the scholar in this lesson? What would change if you committed to the latter?
?
In your own life, can you identify a situation where you confused an accidental cause with an essential one—perhaps focusing on a secondary factor while ignoring the real root? How might this lesson's distinction help you re-evaluate that situation today?
?
The passage shows that heat's role, though real, is only preparatory. What 'heats' in your life—habits, desires, pursuits—do you mistake for the direct cause of your spiritual or moral outcomes, when in fact the essential cause lies elsewhere? Will you investigate that starting this week?

How Cold Produces Sourness
Explains sourness from cold in light substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how cold acting on a light substance produces sourness and why its unpleasantness is intermediate.

Core Idea
Sourness arises from mild condensation in light receptacles.

In this part of the passage, the author explains the origin of sourness as a taste produced by cold acting on light substances. The key is the degree of condensation: lighter condensation yields a milder unpleasantness. This sets the stage for understanding how the same agent can produce different tastes depending on the receptacle.

Guided Path
1
Read the sourness passage
Read the Arabic passage about sourness carefully. Notice how the cold agent condenses the light receptacle and why the resulting unpleasantness is described as 'intermediate'.
The passage includes an example of astringent fruit becoming more sour as it becomes more watery.
2
Understand the mechanism
Identify that sourness results from less condensation compared to dense substances. The cold's effect weakens due to the subtlety of the light receptacle.
The text mentions 'between between' (bain bain) to indicate intermediate unpleasantness.
3
Connect to the example
Reflect on the example of 'al-'afas' fruit: as it becomes more watery and slightly warmed, its sourness increases. This illustrates how a lightening of the substance shifts the taste towards sourness.

Source Text

بسبب النوع الرابع - المقصد الأول : المذوقات وأصولها تكثيفاً أقل بكثير مما في القابل الكثيف، فيحدث فيه كيفية يكون عدم ملاءمتها أقل من عدم ملاءمة العفوصة بكثير أيضاً ، وهي الحموضة وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار بقوله: ) لأنه ( أي الفاعل البارد ( يكثف ( القابل اللطيف ( يبرده ويغوص ) فيه ( بلطافته ) أي لطافته فيضعف فيه تأثيره ) فيكون عدم ملاءمته ) أي عدم ملاءمة الطعم الحادث في ذلك القابل اللطيف (بين بين . ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ الصواب تبديلهما بأقل كما أشرنا إليه (ولذلك ) أي : ولأن الحموضة تحدث من فعل البارد في اللطيف ( كان الثمر (العفص لشدة برده وكثافته ) كلما ازداد مائية ) ولطافة واعتدل قليلا بإسخان الشمس المنضج ) ازداد حموضة و يفعل البارد (في) القابل ( المعتدل قبضاً وهو في عدم الملاءمة ( دون العفوصة) وفوق الحموضة لأنّ تكثيف البرودة في المعتدل أقل من تكثيفها في الكثيف، وأكثر من تكثيفها في اللطيف على قياس ما مر فيحدث فيه كيفية عدم ملاءمتها بين بين وهو القبض وكونه في عدم الملاءمة فوق الحموضة ظاهر، وأما كونه في ذلك دون العفوصة فإليه أشار بقوله : ( إذ العفص يقبض باطن اللسان وظاهره ) معاً فينفر الطبع عنه نفرة شديدة ( والقابض يقبض ظاهره فقط فلا تكون النفرة عنه في تلك الغاية
Type Four - First Aim: Tastes and Their Origins. (It produces) much less condensation than in the dense receptacle, so there arises in it a quality whose unpleasantness is much less than the unpleasantness of astringency also, and it is sourness. And to what we mentioned he pointed with his saying: 'Because it' (i.e., the cold agent) 'condenses the light receptacle, cools it, and penetrates into it with its subtlety, i.e., its subtlety, so its effect weakens in it, so its unpleasantness' (i.e., the unpleasantness of the taste arising in that light receptacle) 'is intermediate.' And it is not hidden from you that the correct thing is to replace them with 'less' as we indicated. 'And therefore' (i.e., because sourness occurs from the action of the cold in the light) 'the astringent fruit (al-'afas), due to its intense cold and density, whenever it increases in wateriness and subtlety and becomes slightly moderate by the heating of the ripening sun, increases in sourness. And the cold acts on the moderate receptacle producing astringency, and it is in unpleasantness below astringency and above sourness, because the condensation of cold in the moderate is less than its condensation in the dense and more than its condensation in the light, on the analogy of what preceded, so there arises in it a quality whose unpleasantness is intermediate, and it is astringency. And its being in unpleasantness above sourness is evident, and as for its being in that below astringency, to that he pointed with his saying: 'Indeed, the astringent (al-'afas) contracts the inner and outer of the tongue together, so nature recoils from it strongly, and the astringent contracts only its outer, so the recoil is not at that extreme.'
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines how the cold element produces two distinct tastes—sourness (hâmidh) and astringency (qâbidh)—depending on the nature of the substance it acts upon. The author begins by explaining sourness: when cold acts on a light (latîf) substance, it condenses it less than it would a dense substance, resulting in a quality whose unpleasantness is intermediate, which is sourness. He then provides an example: the astringent fruit (al-'afas) becomes more sour as it becomes more watery and slightly warmed by the sun. Next, he discusses astringency: when cold acts on a moderate (mu'tadil) substance, the condensation is intermediate—less than in dense, more than in light—yielding a taste whose unpleasantness lies between astringency (higher) and sourness (lower). This is astringency. He clarifies the difference: astringent substances contract both the inner and outer of the tongue, causing strong aversion, while astringent (qâbidh) contracts only the outer, causing less aversion. The passage follows the author's commentary on a base text, explaining the rationale behind the classification.

Key Terms
الحموضة (sourness)
القابل اللطيف (light receptacle)
الفاعل البارد (cold agent)
العفص (astringent fruit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when cold acts on a light receptacle?
A) It produces intense astringency.
B) It produces sourness with intermediate unpleasantness.
C) It produces sweetness.
D) It has no effect.

Key Takeaway
Sourness is produced by cold condensing a light substance, resulting in a moderately unpleasant taste.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you taste something sour, do you ever pause to consider the subtle balance of qualities behind it? This lesson shows that sourness comes from a mild coldness on a light substance. Reflect on a time when your own 'coldness'—whether detachment, harshness, or indifference—produced a 'sour' reaction in a relationship. What 'lightening' or ripening do you need to soften that bitterness?
?
The author corrects a word from 'intermediate' to 'less'. This precision matters. In your own understanding of faith and character, where might you be using comfortable 'intermediate' descriptions for flaws that are actually 'less' than they should be? What would it take to honestly assess and correct your own spiritual condition with such exactness?
?
The astringent fruit becomes sour as it gains wateriness and is heated by the sun. Wateriness represents humility and flexibility; heat represents divine guidance. What dry and rigid areas of your heart need the 'gentle heating' of reflection and the softening of humility to transform your harsh judgments into something more balanced?
